    - Bringing metabolic networks to life: convenience rate law and thermodynamic constraints

        - 
        - # [Bringing metabolic networks to life: convenience rate law and thermodynamic constraints](https://doi.org/10.1186/1742-4682-3-41)
        - 
        - ## [Wolfram Liebermeister; Edda Klipp]()
        - 
        - ## Abstract
        - Background: Translating a known metabolic network into a dynamic model requires rate laws for all chemical reactions.;-The mathematical expressions depend on the underlying enzymatic mechanism↔; they can become quite involved and may contain a large number of parameters. ↔Rate laws and enzyme parameters are still unknown for most enzymes↔.
        - ;-Results: We introduce a simple and general rate law called "convenience kinetics"↔. It can be derived from a simple random-order enzyme mechanism. Thermodynamic laws can impose dependencies on the kinetic parameters. Hence, to facilitate model fitting and parameter optimisation for large networks, we introduce thermodynamically independent system parameters: their values can be varied independently, without violating thermodynamical constraints. ↔We achieve this by expressing the equilibrium constants↔ either by Gibbs free energies of formation or by a set of independent equilibrium constants. The remaining system parameters are mean turnover rates, generalised Michaelis-Menten constants, and constants for inhibition and activation. ↔All parameters correspond to molecular energies↔, for instance, binding energies between reactants and enzyme.
        - Conclusion: Convenience kinetics can be used to translate a biochemical network – manually or automatically - into a dynamical model with plausible biological properties. It implements enzyme saturation and regulation by activators and inhibitors, covers all possible reaction stoichiometries, and can be specified by a small number of parameters. Its mathematical form makes it especially suitable for parameter estimation and optimisation. Parameter estimates can be easily computed from a least-squares fit to Michaelis-Menten values, turnover rates, equilibrium constants, and;-other quantities that are routinely measured in enzyme assays↔ and stored in kinetic databases.
        - 
        - ## Key concepts
        - #claim/#kinetics; #kinetics; #claim/#equilibrium_constant; #equilibrium_constant; #claim/#rate_law; #rate_law; #result/#metabolic_network; #metabolic_network; #claim/#stoichiometries; #stoichiometries; #result/#thermodynamics; #thermodynamics; #claim/#chemical_reaction; #chemical_reaction; #result/#reaction_stoichiometry; #reaction_stoichiometry; #metabolism; #escherichia; #escherichia_coli
        - 
        - ## Quote
        - > The convenience kinetics applies to arbitrary reaction stoichiometries and captures biologically relevant behaviour with a small number of free parameters
        -

        - ## Key points
        - Translating a known metabolic network into a dynamic model requires rate laws for all chemical reactions
        - Parameter estimates can be computed from a least-squares fit to Michaelis-Menten values, turnover rates, equilibrium constants, and other quantities that are routinely measured in enzyme assays and stored in kinetic databases
        - If the denominator is multiplied out, it j contains all mathematical products of normalised substrate concentrations and product concentrations, but no mixed terms containing substrates and products together; the term +1 in the denominator is supposed to appear only once, so it is subtracted in the end
        - The three rate laws differ in their denominators: in convenience kinetics, we find all combinations of substrate concentrations and all combinations of product concentrations, but no mixed terms containing both substrate and product concentrations
        - The convenience kinetics applies to arbitrary reaction stoichiometries and captures biologically relevant behaviour with a small number of free parameters
        - By introducing a set of thermodynamically independent system parameters, we obtained a form of the rate law that ensures thermodynamic correct
        - 
        - ## Synopsis
        - Convenience kinetics can be used to translate a biochemical network into a dynamical model with plausible biological properties.
        - It can be derived from a simple random-order enzyme mechanism and is suitable for parameter estimation and parameter optimisation.
        - <a class="has-tooltip" title="Read the article" target="_blank" href="https://doi.org/10.1186/1742-4682-3-41">Wolfram Klipp (2006)―a> reports that convenience kinetics can be used to translate a biochemical network – manually or automatically - into a dynamical model with plausible biological properties.
        - It implements enzyme saturation and regulation by activators and inhibitors, covers all possible reaction stoichiometries, and can be specified by a small number of parameters.
        - Translating a known metabolic network into a dynamic model requires rate laws for all chemical reactions.
        - Parameter estimates can be computed from a least-squares fit to Michaelis-Menten values.
        - The three rate laws differ in their denominators.<br/><br/>Discussing potential shortcomings, “The convenience kinetics applies to arbitrary reaction stoichiometries and captures biologically relevant behaviour with a small number of free parameters.
        - It represents a simple molecular reaction mechanism in which substrates bind rapidly and in random order to the enzyme without energetic interaction between the binding sites,” they acknowledge. <br /><br />
        - 
        - 
        - ## Summary
        - 
        - ### Introduction
        - Translating a known metabolic network into a dynamic model requires rate laws for all chemical reactions.
        - Rate laws and enzyme parameters are still unknown for most enzymes.
        - Dynamic modelling of biochemical networks requires quantitative information about enzymatic reactions.
        - All reactions could be described by versatile laws such as mass-action kinetics, generalised mass-action kinetics [^3],[^4] or linlog kinetics [^5],[^6].
        - These kinetic laws fail to describe enzyme saturation at high substrate concentrations, which is a common and relevant phenomenon.
        - 
        - ### Methods
        - Basic notions for metabolic models The structure of a metabolic network is defined by the lists of metabolites and reactions and by two structural matrices, N and W.
        - The coefficients nil contained in the stoichiometric matrix N describe how many molecules of type i are produced in reaction l; negative elements describe consumption of molecules.
        - The elements of the regulation matrix W describe enzyme regulation between metabolites i and enzymes l: wli = 1 indicates activation, wli = -1 represents inhibition, and wli = 0 no interaction.
        - The vectors c, v, and k contain the metabolite concentrations, reaction velocities, and system parameters, respectively.
        - External or buffered metabolites with fixed concentrations are contained in the parameter vector k.
        - To relate activation and inhibition to the reaction kinetics, the authors first assume a hypothetical kinetic law without regulation; in this law, the reaction velocity depends only on the substrate and product concentrations.
        - 
        - ### Results
        - The authors introduce a simple and general rate law called "convenience kinetics".
        - It can be derived from a simple random-order enzyme mechanism.
        - To facilitate model fitting and parameter optimisation for large networks, the authors introduce thermodynamically independent system parameters: their values can be varied independently, without violating thermodynamical constraints
        - The authors achieve this by expressing the equilibrium constants either by Gibbs free energies of formation or by a set of independent equilibrium constants.
        - ↔β1 B1 + β2 B2 + ..., the formula looks slightly different: k+cat ∏ aαi i k−cat ∏ b β j j ∏ ∏ v(a,b) = E i.
        - 
        - ### Discussion
        - Convenience kinetics can be used for modelling biochemical systems in a simple and standardised way.
        - In contrast to ad-hoc rate laws such as linlog or generalised massaction kinetics, the convenience kinetics is biochemically justified as a direct generalisation of the Michaelis-Menten kinetics; it is saturable and allows for activation and inhibition of the enzyme.
        - The parameters kM, kA, and kI represent concentrations that lead to half-maximal (or in general, (1 + αi)-1 -maximal) effects: the kM values indicate the threshold between low substrate concentrations that lead to linear kinetics and high concentrations at which the enzyme works in saturation.
        - The convenience kinetics represents a rapid-equilibrium random-order enzyme mechanism.
        - The remaining parameters reflect interaction energies, which depend on the enzyme's amino acid sequence; the authors can expect that these energies vary between cells, and probably more independently than, for instance, the forward and backward turnover rates
        - 
        - ### Conclusion
        - Convenience kinetics can be used to translate a biochemical network – manually or automatically - into a dynamical model with plausible biological properties
        - It implements enzyme saturation and regulation by activators and inhibitors, covers all possible reaction stoichiometries, and can be specified by a small number of parameters.
        - The convenience kinetics applies to arbitrary reaction stoichiometries and captures biologically relevant behaviour with a small number of free parameters.
        - It represents a simple molecular reaction mechanism in which substrates bind rapidly and in random order to the enzyme, without energetic interaction between the binding sites.
        - For reactions with a single substrate and a single product, the convenience kinetics equals the well-known Michaelis-Menten kinetics.
        - By introducing a set of thermodynamically independent system parameters, the authors obtained a form of the rate law that ensures thermodynamic correct-
        -

        - ## Builds on previous research
        - Convenience kinetics represents a random-order enzyme mechanism Like many established rate laws (first of all, irreversible Michaelis-Menten kinetics [^20]), convenience kinetics can be derived from a molecular enzyme mechanism.;-We impose three main assumptions: (i) the substrates bind to the enzyme in arbitrary order and are converted into the products, which then dissociate from the enzyme in arbitrary order; (ii) binding of substrates and products is reversible and much faster than the conversion step; (iii) the binding energies of individual reactants do not depend on other reactants already bound to the enzyme↔
        - Besides the turnover rates and kM values, their kinetic laws also contain product inhibition constants.;-For the comparison, we made the simplifying↔ (yet biologically realistic) assumption that these inhibition constants equal the respective kM values, which yields the following rate laws [^8]
        - Equations (36) and (37) constitute the relation (18) between equilibrium constants and the Gibbs free energies of formation.;-Selection of independent equilibrium constants Dependencies between equilibrium constants can be treated in a similar manner to the linear dependencies that constitute the conservation relations between metabolites↔ [^25]
        - 
        - ## Differs from previous work
        - The single terms reflect the reactant complexes formed by the enzyme.;-The second concern of this paper was the incorporation of thermodynamic constraints↔: in pathway-based methods [^21],[^22],[^23], proper treatment of the Gibbs free energies yields constraints on the flux directions; in our kinetic models, it leads to linear dependencies between the logarithmic parameters
        - 
        - ## Contributions
        - Convenience kinetics can be used to translate a biochemical network – manually or automatically - into a dynamical model with plausible biological properties. It implements enzyme saturation and regulation by activators and inhibitors, covers all possible reaction stoichiometries, and can be specified by a small number of parameters. Its mathematical form makes it especially suitable for parameter estimation and optimisation. Parameter estimates can be easily computed from a least-squares fit to Michaelis-Menten values, turnover rates, equilibrium constants, and other quantities that are routinely measured in enzyme assays and stored in kinetic databases.
        - 
        - ## Limitations
        - It implements enzyme saturation and regulation by activators and inhibitors, covers all possible reaction stoichiometries, and can be specified by a small number of parameters. Its mathematical form makes it especially suitable for parameter estimation and optimisation. Parameter estimates can be easily computed from a least-squares fit to Michaelis-Menten values, turnover rates, equilibrium constants, and other quantities that are routinely measured in enzyme assays and stored in kinetic databases.
        - The convenience kinetics applies to arbitrary reaction stoichiometries and captures biologically relevant behaviour (saturation, activation, inhibition) with a small number of free parameters. It represents a simple molecular reaction mechanism in which substrates bind rapidly and in random order to the enzyme, without energetic interaction between the binding sites. The same holds for the dissociation of products.
        -

        - # 
        - 
    - Bringing metabolic networks to life: integration of kinetic, metabolic, and proteomic data
        - [Klipp_BringingMetabolicNetworksLifeIntegration_2006]() 
        - 
        - # [Bringing metabolic networks to life: integration of kinetic, metabolic, and proteomic data](https://doi.org/10.1186/1742-4682-3-42)
        - 
        - ## [Wolfram Liebermeister; Edda Klipp]()
        - 
        - ## Abstract
        - Background: Translating a known metabolic network into a dynamic model requires reasonable guesses of all enzyme parameters. In Bayesian parameter estimation, model parameters are described by a posterior probability distribution, which scores the potential parameter sets, showing how well each of them agrees with the data and with the prior assumptions made.
        - Results: We compute posterior distributions of kinetic parameters within a Bayesian framework, based on integration of kinetic, thermodynamic, metabolic, and proteomic data. The structure of the metabolic system (i.e., stoichiometries and enzyme regulation) needs to be known, and the reactions are modelled by convenience kinetics with thermodynamically independent parameters. The parameter posterior is computed in two separate steps: a first posterior summarises the available data on enzyme kinetic parameters; an improved second posterior is obtained by integrating metabolic fluxes, concentrations, and enzyme concentrations for one or more steady states. The data can be heterogenous, incomplete, and uncertain, and the posterior is approximated by a multivariate log-normal distribution. We apply the method to a model of the threonine synthesis pathway: the integration of metabolic data has little effect on the marginal posterior distributions of individual model parameters. Nevertheless, it leads to strong correlations between the parameters in the joint posterior distribution, which greatly improve the model predictions by the following Monte-Carlo simulations.
        - ;-Conclusion: We present a standardised method to translate metabolic networks into dynamic models↔. To determine the model parameters, evidence from various experimental data is combined and weighted using Bayesian parameter estimation. The resulting posterior parameter distribution describes a statistical ensemble of parameter sets; the parameter variances and correlations can account for missing knowledge, measurement uncertainties, or biological variability. The posterior distribution can be used to sample model instances and to obtain probabilistic statements about the model's dynamic behaviour.
        - 
        - ## Key concepts
        - #claim/#kinetics; #kinetics; #result/#threonine; #threonine; #claim/#model_parameter; #model_parameter; #result/#steady_state; #steady_state; #claim/#posterior_distribution; #posterior_distribution; #claim/#metabolic_network; #metabolic_network; #result/#metabolic_flux; #metabolic_flux; #biology; #result/#normal_distribution; #normal_distribution; #aspartate_kinase; #result/#metabolic_system; #metabolic_system; #homoserine_kinase; #threonine_synthase; #aspartate_semialdehyde_dehydrogenase; #escherichia; #claim/#dynamic_model; #dynamic_model; #escherichia_coli; #saccharomyces_cerevisiae; #posterior_probability_distribution
        - 
        - ## Quote
        - > The chemical reactions are catalysed by aspartate kinase, aspartate semialdehyde dehydrogenase, homoserine dehydrogenase, homoserine kinase, and threonine synthase
        - 
        - 
        - ## Figures
        - ![](https://scholarcy.nyc3.digitaloceanspaces.com/images/114191/1742-4682-3-42/img-000.png) 
        - ![](https://scholarcy.nyc3.digitaloceanspaces.com/images/114191/1742-4682-3-42/img-005.png) 
        - ## Key points
        - Translating a known metabolic network into a dynamic model requires reasonable guesses of all enzyme parameters
        - The resulting posterior parameter distribution describes a statistical ensemble of parameter sets; the parameter variances and correlations can account for missing knowledge, measurement uncertainties, or biological variability
        - We proposed a method to construct kinetic models from biochemical networks: all reactions are modelled by convenience kinetics, and the parameters are characterised by a posterior distribution
        - We have presented a method to guess model parameters by integrating existing kinetic, metabolic, and proteomic data
        - The estimation procedure can be split into two separate steps: first, the kinetic parameters in the model are fitted to kinetic and thermodynamic data; second, the parameters are improved by fitting them to metabolic steady states
        - The chemical reactions are catalysed by aspartate kinase (AK), aspartate semialdehyde dehydrogenase (ASD), homoserine dehydrogenase (HDH), homoserine kinase (HK), and threonine synthase (TSY)
        - 
        - 
        - ## Summary
        - 
        - ### Introduction
        - Translating a known metabolic network into a dynamic model requires reasonable guesses of all enzyme parameters.
        - The structure of metabolic networks has been characterised for many organisms [^1],[^2],[^3], and metabolic fluxes in large networks [^4],[^5],[^6] are successfully described by pathway- or constraint-based methods [710]
        - Such methods do not explain how the fluxes are evoked by the activities of enzymes and how they respond to moderate perturbations.
        - These questions can be answered by kinetic models, which employ differential equations to describe the temporal behaviour of the system.
        - Parameter estimation is an integral part of kinetic modelling, and model fitting is currently receiving increasing attention [^25],[^26],[^27],[^28],[^29]
        - 
        - ### Methods
        - Empirical distributions of kinetic parameters The authors obtained prior distributions for different types of parameters from statistics over experimental data [^18][^19],[^20],[^23],[^24].
        - The results are shown in table 1.
        - 1. Experimental values for turnover rates, substrate, product, and inhibition constants were taken from the Brenda database [^18].
        - The database contains multiple values for some of the parameters; the authors counted them separately.
        - 2. To obtain energy constants, the authors used Gibbs free energies of formation predicted from the molecule structures, using the group contribution method [^23]: values for CoA-complexes were neglected in the statistics, and the values for the remaining compounds were -590 ± 447 J/ mol.
        - 
        - ### Results
        - The authors compute posterior distributions of kinetic parameters within a Bayesian framework, based on integration of kinetic, thermodynamic, metabolic, and proteomic data.
        - The parameter posterior is computed in two separate steps: a first posterior summarises the available data on enzyme kinetic parameters; an improved second posterior is obtained by integrating metabolic fluxes, concentrations, and enzyme concentrations for one or more steady states.
        - The authors apply the method to a model of the threonine synthesis pathway: the integration of metabolic data has little effect on the marginal posterior distributions of individual model parameters.
        - It leads to strong correlations between the parameters in the joint posterior distribution, which greatly improve the model predictions by the following Monte-Carlo simulations
        - 
        - ### Discussion
        - The authors proposed a method to construct kinetic models from biochemical networks: all reactions are modelled by convenience kinetics, and the parameters are characterised by a posterior distribution.
        - Other kinetic laws can be used just as well if the kinetic parameters can be expressed by thermodynamically independent parameters that obey an equation of form (8)
        - This holds for many kinetic laws including mass-action kinetics and laws of the Michaelis-Menten type.
        - Parameters such as activation and inhibition constants, which do not affect the chemical equilibrium, can be chosen independently.
        - The posterior distribution represents a compromise between the typical ranges of model parameters and a fit to specific experimental data.
        - If new data become available, the model parameters can be reestimated, using the old posterior distribution as a prior for the parameter fit.
        - It is possible to use the goal function (9) with other parameter estimation algorithms
        - 
        - ### Conclusion
        - The authors present a standardised method to translate metabolic networks into dynamic models.
        - To simulate a biochemical system, the network structure, the kinetic laws, and the kinetic parameters must be determined.
        - This process involves literature studies and several iteration cycles of experiments, parameter fitting, and model selection.
        - The authors have presented a method to guess model parameters by integrating existing kinetic, metabolic, and proteomic data.
        - The parameters are described by a posterior parameter distribution that summarises the information extracted from the experimental data.
        - The covariance matrix describes the remaining uncertainties and the correlations between the parameters; by sampling from the parameter distribution, the authors can simulate more and more model instances and explore their behaviour.
        - If the parameter distribution is narrow, metabolic concentrations and fluxes deviate little from the typical behaviour, and their distribution can be approximated by analytical calculation [^15]
        - 
        - ## Data analysis
        - #method/nonlinear_model
        - #method/gaussian_distributions
        - #method/gaussian_distribution
        - 
        - ## Builds on previous research
        - The posterior summarises all information that has been put into the model and can be used to provide parameter ranges for further modelling, to sample model instances [^30],[^32], or to predict confidence intervals of steady state fluxes and concentrations or responses to differential expression [^15]. Even if the data do not suffice for an exact parameter;-fit, we will still obtain a model↔; the uncertainty of the parameters and correlations between them can be read directly from the posterior parameter distribution.
        - Parameter estimation Bayesian parameter estimation [^35] integrates two sources of knowledge: (i) expectations about the model parameters are quantified by a prior probability density p(θ). The prior can describe typical parameter ranges or summarise;-the results of earlier experiments; (ii) the support by experimental data is quantified by the likelihood function p↔(x*|θ)
        - With the convenience kinetics, the;-corresponding vector x of model predictions is a linear function of θkin, which greatly simplifies the calculation [^31]. In the second step, the parameter estimates are further improved by a fit to metabolite concentrations, metabolic fluxes, and protein concentrations from one or more steady states; we shall summarize them here as "metabolic data" and collect them in a vector y*↔
        - Thus, for the energy constants ln kiG = Gi/(RT) in the threonine model, we chose a different prior, with a mean value of zero and a standard deviation of In 200 ≈ 5.3. Empirical distributions of kinetic parameters;-We obtained prior distributions for different types of parameters from statistics over experimental data↔ [^18][^19],[^20],[^23],[^24]
        - 
        - ## Differs from previous work
        - [http://www.tbiomed.com/content/3/1/42](http://www.tbiomed.com/content/3/1/42) as many data as possible: besides thermodynamic and kinetic parameters, we also integrate proteome data and metabolic concentrations and fluxes (see Figure 1). As the data are incomplete and unreliable,;-we do not describe the model parameters by sharp values↔, but by a joint posterior distribution [^15]
        - Computing the posterior distribution Theoretically, we can obtain the posterior distribution p(θ|x*, y*) by inserting the distributions (5), (6), and (7) into (4). But how can;-we actually compute it? Standard methods for sampling the posterior distribution, such as Gibbs sampling [^35], become unfeasible if the number of [http://www.tbiomed.com/content/3/1/42](http://www.tbiomed.com/content/3/1/42) measured parameter x x( θ) likelihood true x observed x*↔
        - 
        - ## Contributions
        - We present a standardised method to translate metabolic networks into dynamic models. To determine the model parameters, evidence from various experimental data is combined and weighted using Bayesian parameter estimation. The resulting posterior parameter distribution describes a statistical ensemble of parameter sets; the parameter variances and correlations can account for missing knowledge, measurement uncertainties, or biological variability. The posterior distribution can be used to sample model instances and to obtain probabilistic statements about the model's dynamic behaviour.<br/><br/>(page number not for citation purposes)<br/><br/>Theoretical Biology and Medical Modelling 2006, 3:42
        - 
        - ## Limitations
        - Our approach is limited by the two approximations made: (i) the true reaction kinetics are replaced by convenience kinetics; (ii) to compute the posterior, the model is linearised around a posterior mode. Nevertheless, automatic parameter estimation can provide reasonable first guesses and plausible ranges of model parameters. Kinetic parameters obtained from the integration of many literature values and incorporation of thermodynamic constraints are probably more reliable than the single literature values.
        - 
        - ## Data and code
        - If only relative data are available, appropriate scaling factors have to be estimated along with the other model parameters
        -

    - Kinetic Modeling of the Genetic Information Processes in a Minimal Cell
        - [Thornburg_et+al_KineticModelingGeneticInformationProcesses_2019]() 
        - 
        - # [Kinetic Modeling of the Genetic Information Processes in a Minimal Cell](https://doi.org/10.3389/fmolb.2019.00130)
        - 
        - ## [Zane R. Thornburg](); [Marcelo C. R. Melo](); [David Bianchi]() et al.
        - 
        - ## Abstract
        - JCVI-syn3A is a minimal bacterial cell with a 543 kbp genome consisting of 493 genes. For this slow growing minimal cell with a 105 min doubling time, we recently established the essential metabolism including the transport of required nutrients from the environment, the gene map, and genome-wide proteomics. Of the 452 protein-coding genes, 143 are assigned to metabolism and 212 are assigned to genetic information processing.;-Using genome-wide proteomics and experimentally measured kinetic parameters from the literature we present here kinetic models for the genetic information processes of DNA replication, replication initiation, transcription, and translation which are solved stochastically and averaged over 1,000 replicates/cells↔. The model predicts the time required for replication initiation and DNA replication to be 8 and 50 min on average respectively and the number of proteins and ribosomal components to be approximately doubled in a cell cycle. The model of genetic information processing when combined with the essential metabolic and cell growth networks will provide a powerful platform for studying the fundamental principles of life.
        - 
        - ## Key concepts
        - #claim/#mRNA; #mRNA; #claim/#transcription; #transcription; #claim/#translation; #translation; #claim/#ribosomal_protein; #ribosomal_protein; #claim/#cell_cycle; #cell_cycle; #claim/#proteomics; #proteomics; #claim/#ribosome; #ribosome; #claim/#dna_replication; #dna_replication; #claim/#ribosomal_rna; #ribosomal_rna; #RNAP; #metabolism; #escherichia; #escherichia_coli; #biology; #gene_map; #biogenesis; #protein_production; #claim/#mycoplasma_pneumoniae; #mycoplasma_pneumoniae
        - 
        - ## Quote
        - > A higher transcription rate was justified from the greater promoter strength of the ribosomal RNA operon observed in E. coli and other bacteria as well as the presence of multiple RNAPs estimated to be reading the operon
        - 
        -

        - ## Key points
        - In our previous work on ribosome biogenesis in Escherichia coli ([^Earnest_et+al_2015_a], [^Earnest_et+al_2016_a]), ribosome assembly was included along with DNA replication and transcription/translation of just the ribosomal proteins. In this simplified model we focus on developing kinetic parameters that replicate the DNA, generate proteins comparable to the proteomics abundances, and produce sufficient numbers of rprotein and ribosomal RNA to generate approximately 500–700 ribosomes estimated from the biomass equation in [^Breuer_et+al_2019_a])
        - The average number of any mRNA is within the expected range from zero to ten as reported in E. coli ([^Milo_2015_a]) and can be used as predictions for mRNA counts in JCVI-syn3A until transcriptomic data or smFISH experiments are available for validation
        - In the case of ribosomal RNA (rRNA), a higher transcription rate generated a sufficient number of rRNA to form 500–700 ribosomes in a cell cycle
        - A higher transcription rate was justified from the greater promoter strength of the rRNA operon observed in E. coli and other bacteria ([^Maeda_et+al_2015_a]) as well as the presence of multiple RNAPs estimated to be reading the operon ([^Bremer_2008_a])
        - In the case of non-ribosomal proteins where we assigned promoter strengths based on proteomics counts, our model, to the most part, approximately doubles the number of proteins over a cell cycle
        - This neglect can be justified by assumed timescale separation of the processes, but in general more experimental measurements of the assembly reactions would help to establish to what degree the association of the complexes are captured in the kinetic parameters given in the literature for the fundamental processes of replication, transcription, and translation
        - 
        - ## Synopsis
        - A model of genetic information processing has been developed for the first time in a single cell, the JCVI-syn3A cell,.
        - In ‘Kinetic Modeling of the Genetic Information Processes in a Minimal Cell’, <a class="has-tooltip" title="Read the article" target="_blank" href="https://doi.org/10.3389/fmolb.2019.00130">a research team at the Department of Chemistry led by Zane Thornburg (2019)―a> reported that In the previous work on ribosome biogenesis in Escherichia coli, ribosom assembly was included along with DNA replication and transcription/translation of just the ribosomal proteins.
        - In this simplified model they focus on developing kinetic parameters that replicate the DNA, generate proteins comparable to the proteomics abundances, and produce sufficient numbers of rprotein and Ribosomal RNA.
        - The average number of any mRNA is within the expected range from zero to ten.
        - Each of the genetic information processing subsystems involve species that are low in population in the cell.
        - The kinetics were modeled with chemical master equation (CME) simulations and solved using the Gillespie algorithm.
        - The results of stochastic simulations were averaged over 1,000 replicates/cells.<br/><br/>JCVI-syn3A is a minimal bacterial cell with a 543 kbp genome consisting of 493 genes.
        - For this slow growing minimal cell, they recently established the essential metabolism including transport of required nutrients from the environment.<br /><br />The findings appear to confirm earlier work in this field: “The average number of any mRNA is within the expected range from zero to ten as reported in E. coli.
        - We predict that approximately 450 messengers will be present in the cell on average, agreeing with the extrapolated number for a 400 nm diameter cell,” Thornburg claimed. <br /><br />Data and code to reproduce the analyses can be found at: <a target="_blank" href="https://github.com/zanert2/Thornburg_">https://github.com/zanert2/Thornburg_―a>.
        - 
        - 
        - ## Summary
        - 
        - ### Introduction
        - JCVI-syn3A, a bacterial cell with a synthetic minimal genome of size 543 kbp and 493 genes, is an organism designed to have the fewest genes necessary for life and is an ideal model organism for studying fundamental principles of life ([^Lachance_et+al_2019_a]).
        - In Breuer et al (2019), the authors published the flux balance analysis of the essential metabolism of JCVI-syn3A along with the gene map and the genome-wide data from essentiality and proteomics experiments.
        - Metabolism, including transport of nutrients into the cell, has been established, the reactions and kinetic models for genetic information processes in JCVI-syn3A are missing.
        - The model presented here uses the proteomics data to guide the modeling of protein production
        - 
        - ### Methods
        - Each of the genetic information processing subsystems involve species that are low in population in the cell, for example one or two copies of a gene and 0–10 copies of a protein-coding mRNA.
        - To capture the stochastic nature of genetic information processes, the kinetics were modeled with chemical master equation (CME) simulations and solved using the Gillespie algorithm as implemented in the software Lattice Microbes ([^Roberts_et+al_2013_a]; [^Hallock_et+al_2014_a]; [^Earnest_et+al_2015_a], [^Earnest_et+al_2018_a]) with the pyLM interface in a Python 3 Jupyter notebook.
        - The results of stochastic simulations were averaged over 1,000 replicates/cells.
        - Each replicate requires a run time of one second.
        - The Jupyter notebooks are available and are posted at GitHub
        - 
        - ### Results
        - The authors predict replication initiation and replication are completed by 65 min, leaving another 40 min for the cell to divide in the 105 min cell cycle
        - 
        - ### Conclusion
        - The authors' detailed model for the initiation of DNA replication builds upon observations from crystal structures of the initiator protein DnaA bound to signatures on ds-and ssDNA found near the oriC and smFRET measurements of the DnaA filament formation on ssDNA.
        - In the case of rRNA, a higher transcription rate generated a sufficient number of rRNA to form 500–700 ribosomes in a cell cycle.
        - The long ribosomal proteins were not generated quickly enough and the shorter ribosomal proteins occurred in much higher numbers
        - This is likely due to no promoter strength being assigned to the transcription of genes coding for ribosomal proteins.
        - In the case of non-ribosomal proteins where the authors assigned promoter strengths based on proteomics counts, the model, to the most part, approximately doubles the number of proteins over a cell cycle.
        - Identification of the promoter sequences and operonal structures for genes in JCVI-syn3A would help assign variation in promoter strengths and transcription rates on the basis of genomic information rather than proteomics values
        - 
        - 
        - ## Study subjects
        - 
        - ### 493 genes
        - .;-JCVI-syn3A is a minimal bacterial cell with a 543 kbp genome consisting of 493 genes↔. For this slow growing minimal cell with a 105 min doubling time, we recently established the essential metabolism including the transport of required nutrients from the environment, the gene map, and genome-wide proteomics
        - 
        - ### 452 protein-coding genes
        - For this slow growing minimal cell with a 105 min doubling time, we recently established the essential metabolism including the transport of required nutrients from the environment, the gene map, and genome-wide proteomics.;-Of the 452 protein-coding genes, 143 are assigned to metabolism and 212 are assigned to genetic information processing↔. Using genome-wide proteomics and experimentally measured kinetic parameters from the literature we present here kinetic models for the genetic information processes of DNA replication, replication initiation, transcription, and translation which are solved stochastically and averaged over 1,000 replicates/cells
        - 
        - ### 452 protein coding genes
        - Within the cell cycle of 105 min, these processes duplicate the genome, generate, and translate sufficient amounts of mRNA to approximately reproduce the proteomics data, and the estimated number of ribosomes.;-All 452 protein coding genes and 35 genes for rRNAs and tRNAs in the genome of JCVI-syn3A are expressed↔. Three pseudo genes and three genes for small RNA are not expressed in this model
        - 
        - ## Data analysis
        - #method/gillespie_algorithm
        - 
        - ## Confirmation of earlier findings
        - The association constant for nucleotides binding to the RNAP/gene complex KD was fitted so that the rate of transcription was maximized by making transcript length the dominant term in the denominator of k in Equation (3).―Our fitted value agrees with a measured experimental value of 0↔.14 mM ([^Larson_et+al_2012_a]↔a>)
        - The average number of any mRNA is within the expected range from zero to ten as reported in E. coli ([^Milo_2015_a]) and can be used as predictions for mRNA counts in JCVI-syn3A until transcriptomic data or smFISH experiments are available for validation.;-We predict that approximately 450 messengers will be present in the cell on average, agreeing with the extrapolated number for a 400 nm diameter cell from↔ [^Lynch_2015_a])

        - ## Data and code
        - DATA AVAILABILITY STATEMENT The jupyter notebooks containing the models in this study can be found at [https://github.com/zanert2/Thornburg_](https://github.com/zanert2/Thornburg_) FrontMolBiosci_2019. AUTHOR CONTRIBUTIONS ZT and ZL-S:developed models for genetic information processes, data curation, writing—original draft, and writing— reviewing and editing. MM:assistance in writing of Jupyter notebooks. DB and TB:advised Lattice Microbes interface for the stochastic model. HS:assisted in development of DNA replication initiation model. CC:constructed initial stochastic model of replication initiation. MB:data curation. CH and JG:reviewing.

        - ## References
        - [^Schaper_et+al_1997_a]: Schaper and Messer, 1995; Weigel et al., 1997 Schaper and Messer, 1995; Weigel et al., 1997 Cheng et al., 2014 Cheng et al., 2014 the genome within the 105 min doubling time, the choice of KD was made in order to minimize the time to duplicate the DNA. Assuming the constant pool sizes and DNAP concentrations, the value of KD corresponds to the value where the length of the genome is the dominant term in the denominator of k in Equation (3).         - [^To_2008_a]: To modify the general rate form for transcription, we incorporate two factors: the probability of an active RNAP selecting any gene Pgene selection and the strength of the gene’s promoter Spromoter. The fraction of active RNAP as estimated by Bremer and Dennis (2008) for a cell with a ∼100 min doubling time implies that around 29 of the 187 RNAP are actively transcribing at any time. Of the actively transcribing RNAPs, Bremer and Dennis (2008) estimate that approximately 24% are involved in making stable RNA like rRNA. Since each rRNA operon only contains the 16S, 23S, and 5S rRNAs and no tRNAs, transcription of the two drRNA genes will require four RNAP. Therefore, the probability of any other gene being selected is Pgene selection = 25/487 = 0.05.     - Fundamental behaviors emerge from simulations of a living minimal cell
        - [Thornburg_et+al_FundamentalBehaviorsEmergeFromSimulations_2022]() 
        - 
        - # [Fundamental behaviors emerge from simulations of a living minimal cell](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cell.2021.12.025)
            - Introduction
                - **Introduction**
                    - Central aim of molecular biology: Explaining basic processes of life using laws of physics and chemistry.
                    - Morowitz's 1984 proposal: Study of Mycoplasmas, the simplest living cells, as models for understanding life's fundamental principles (Morowitz, 1984).
                - **Study of Minimal Cells**
                    - The concept: Similar to how the study of hydrogen (the simplest atom) led to understanding more complex atoms, studying the simplest living cells could reveal principles applicable to all living systems.
                    - Approach: Designing and building cellular genomes without non-essential genes in the lab.
                    - Accomplishments: Have produced living cells with fewer genes than any known naturally occurring cell (Hutchison et al., 2016; Breuer et al., 2019).
                - **Purpose of Minimal Cells**
                    - Ease of description: These cells should be simpler to completely describe than any known naturally occurring cells.
                    - Exploring life's question: Testing whether the combined functions of the minimal cell genes can inform a computer model predicting the cell's behavior.
                    - Testing understanding: This provides a test of our understanding of the minimal requirements for life.
                    - Design tool: Such a model will also provide a tool for predicting the effects of changes in the genome.
                - **Describing the State of a Cell**
                    - Requirements: Knowledge of its size, shape, components, intracellular reactions, and interactions with its environment over time and cell growth.
                    - Additional needs: Theoretical models and simulations to interpret and integrate the experimental data.
                - **Challenges in Simulating a Cell**
                    - Obstacles: Large numbers of genes with unknown function, complexity in model systems, broad range of concentrations and timescales.
                    - Evolution of Whole-cell Models (WCMs): Progressed from genome-scale metabolic models (GSMMs) (Varma and Palsson, 1994), recent reviews on the topic (Goldberg et al., 2018; Marucci et al., 2020).
                    - Comprehensive models: Developed for Mycoplasma genitalium and E. coli (Karr et al., 2012; Macklin et al., 2020), treated in terms of ordinary differential equations, flux balance analysis, and stochastic simulations.
                    - Common challenges: Establishing the reaction networks, availability of kinetic parameters and -omics data (metabolomics and proteomics) to use as initial conditions.
                - **Development of a Whole-cell Model (WCM)**
                    - Information synthesis: No single bacterium has a complete set of parameters and -omics data, so development of a WCM relies on synthesizing information from other well-studied organisms.
                    - Unique approach: Unlike previous WCMs, the simulations presented in this study are based on fully dynamical kinetic models where subsystem networks and chemical species are interconnected continuously over time on a single-cell basis.
                    - 
                - **Ideal System for Whole-cell Model**
                    - Concept: An ideal system would be a minimal cell with as few genes and reactions as possible for the cell to grow and divide (Luthey-Schulten, 2021).
                - **JCVIsyn3A**
                    - Definition: A genetically minimal bacterial cell, consisting of 493 genes on a single 543-kbp circular chromosome with 452 genes coding for proteins (Breuer et al., 2019). Some of these code for subunits of multi-domain complexes (NCBI GenBank: CP016816.2).
                    - Comparison: Syn3A’s genome and physical size are approximately one-tenth those of E. coli.
                    - Clarity: Syn3A has a smaller fraction of genes with unclear function (20%) than E. coli (38%) and Mycoplasma pneumoniae (45%) (Breuer et al., 2019).
                - **Opportunity Presented by Syn3A**
                    - Reduction in complexity and scale of Syn3A presents a unique opportunity to develop a near-complete whole-cell kinetic model.
                - **Syn3A Genome**
                    - Synthesis: The Syn3A genome was synthesized based on the known genome sequence of the natural parent Gram-positive organism Mycoplasma mycoides subsp. capri str. GM12 (GenBank: CP001621.1).
                    - Achievements: It has been synthetically reduced to achieve a minimal genome producing living cells that grow and divide in about 100 min, with consistent spherical morphologies with 400–500-nm diameters (Breuer et al., 2019; Pelletier et al., 2021; Hutchison et al., 2016; Gibson et al., 2010).
                - **Establishment of Essential GSMM for Syn3A**
                    - Essential GSMM: In Breuer et al. (2019), we established the essential Genome-scale metabolic models (GSMM) for Syn3A along with genome-wide gene essentiality and proteomics.
                    - Subsystems: The protein products of 155 genes involved in 175 metabolic reactions were organized into seven subsystems: central, nucleotide, lipid, cofactors, amino acid, ions, and macromolecule metabolism.
                - **Kinetic Metabolic Model**
                    - Model: The reactions in macromolecule metabolism are now kinetically modeled (Thornburg et al., 2019) through approximately 2,000 reactions involving the 251 genes in the genetic information processes of DNA replication, transcription, translation, and degradation of all mRNA, tRNA charging, and cell growth.
                - **Whole-cell, 3D Spatial Models**
                    - Requirement: These models require cellular architecture, including spatial distributions of ribosomes and configurations of the circular chromosome.
                    - Cellular architectures: These are reconstructed at the single-cell level directly from cryo-electron tomograms (cryo-ET) that reveal a near-random distribution of ribosomes throughout the cell with a few present in polysomes (Gilbert et al., 2021).
                - **Kinetic Parameters**
                    - Source: Kinetic parameters for the majority of the cellular reactions have been measured in related organisms through decades of biochemical, single-molecule (sm) FRET, and spectroscopic studies reported in the literature and kinetic databases like Bremer and Dennis (2008), BRENDA (Chang et al., 2021), and equilibrium constants reported in NIST’s TECRdb (Goldberg et al., 2004) and Equilibrator (Flamholz et al., 2012).
                - **Comparative Proteomics Analyses**
                    - Purpose: Comparative proteomics analyses were used to approximate missing or questionable information regarding a few of the Syn3A enzymes.
                    - Comparative proteomics analyses were carried out with Mesoplasma florum (Matteau et al., 2020; Lachance et al., 2021), B. subtilis (Wang et al., 2015), and E. coli (Taniguchi et al., 2010).
                    - Limitation: At the moment, only relative metabolomics data on Syn3A is available, so the metabolite concentrations used to initialize the simulations of the Syn3A WCM were estimated from a scaling of the comprehensive study done on E. coli (Park et al., 2016) and a limited list from M. pneumoniae (Yus et al., 2009).
                - **Whole-cell Kinetic Model Development**
                    - The background data now available for Syn3A allowed for the development of a whole-cell kinetic model of this minimal cell.
                    - Due to large variation in timescales and concentrations, developing a whole-cell model that treats metabolism, genetic information processes, and growth can only be achieved at the moment by hybrid stochastic and deterministic simulations.
                    - 
                - **Kinetics of Essential Metabolic Network**
                    - Kinetics of the essential metabolic network (Breuer et al., 2019) are handled deterministically via ordinary differential equations (ODEs).
                    - 
                - **Kinetics of Genetic Information Processes**
                    - Kinetics of the genetic information processes are handled with stochastic simulations.
                    - Two models of the stochastic simulations are considered:
                        - A chemical master equation (CME) description that assumes that the whole cell is well stirred and implicitly includes the effects of diffusion in the rates of transcription, mRNA degradation, and translation.
                        - A reaction-diffusion master equation (RDME) description that requires macromolecules to diffuse to each other for reactions to take place in the spatially heterogeneous environment of the cell.
                        - 
                - **Simulation Recording**
                    - In the simulations, time-dependent particle counts of each molecule and intermediate, fluxes of all metabolic reactions, and the position of each macromolecule within the cell are recorded.
                    - 
                - **Results Presentation**
                    - Results of the well-stirred model over a complete cell cycle for 174 healthy replicate cells out of a total population of 207 cells are presented.
                    - Unhealthy cells within the simulations run out of phosphoenol pyruvate (pep), halting glycolysis.
                    - 
                - **Spatial Model**
                    - For the spatial model, which is computationally expensive, the team used GPU-based Lattice Microbes software (Roberts et al., 2013; Hallock et al., 2014; Earnest et al., 2018) to simulate replicates over the first 20 min of the cell cycle, before DNA replication and substantial cell growth occurred.
                    - These relatively short-term simulations are critical in calculating binding probabilities and estimating mRNA half-lives in the well-stirred model.
                    - 
                - **Well-Stirred Model**
                    - The well-stirred model, being computationally less expensive, runs on CPUs and provides a whole-cell model that can be easily complexified to allow addition of more pathways and testing.
                    - It helps to quantitatively understand the "principles of life" for a minimal cell growing with little regulation.
                    - 
            - Results
                - 3D spatial whole-cell simulations incorporate experimental data and inform homogeneous well-stirred simulations
                - The kinetic model is influenced by the defined medium composition and new genome annotations
                - Timing of the cell cycle and cell growth are determined by dynamics of DNA replication and surface area growth
                - Balance of genetic information processes and metabolism
                - Time-dependent ATP costs in the minimal cell quantify the cell’s significant energy costs
                - Time-dependent concentrations show consistent average behavior and large population variability
                - Agreement between hybrid well-stirred and spatial simulations
            - Discussion
                - Limitations of the study

        - 
        - ## [Zane R. Thornburg](); [David M. Bianchi](); [Troy A. Brier]() et al.
        - 
        - ## Abstract
            - We present a whole-cell fully dynamical kinetic model (WCM) of JCVI-syn3A, a minimal cell with a reduced genome of 493 genes that has retained few regulatory proteins or small RNAs;-. Cryo-electron tomograms provide the cell geometry and ribosome distributions. ↔Time-dependent behaviors of concentrations and reaction fluxes from stochastic-deterministic simulations over a cell cycle reveal how the cell balances demands of its metabolism↔, genetic information processes, and growth, and offer insight into the principles of life for this minimal cell. The energy economy of each process including active transport of amino acids, nucleosides, and ions is analyzed. ↔WCM reveals how emergent imbalances lead to slowdowns↔ in the rates of transcription and translation. ↔Integration of experimental data is critical in building a kinetic model from which emerges a genome-wide distribution of mRNA half-lives↔, ↔multiple DNA replication events that can be compared to qPCR results↔, and the experimentally observed doubling behavior.
        -

            - Cryo electron tomograms
                - Cryo-electron tomography (cryo-ET) is a label-free cryogenic imaging technique that provides 3D datasets of organelles and protein complexes at nanometer resolution. This is done by opening windows into the cell with focused ion beam (FIB) milling of a cryogenically frozen (vitrified) cell¹. Cryo-electron tomography provides 3D snapshots of proteins at work within their functional cellular environments, allowing users to see and understand how they, and other molecules, work together to carry out major processes in a cell¹.

                - What are some other cryogenic imaging techniques?

                    - Some other cryogenic imaging techniques include Cryogenic transmission electron microscopy (cryo-TEM), which is a transmission electron microscopy technique that is used in structural biology and materials science¹. Electron crystallography is another method to determine the arrangement of atoms in solids using a TEM¹. Cryogenic electron microscopy (cryo-EM) is a cryomicroscopy technique applied on samples cooled to cryogenic temperatures¹. For biological specimens, the structure is preserved by embedding in an environment of vitreous ice¹.

            - Pyruvate kinase
                - Pyruvate kinase is the enzyme involved in the last step of glycolysis. It catalyzes the transfer of a phosphate group from phosphoenolpyruvate (PEP) to adenosine diphosphate (ADP), yielding one molecule of pyruvate and one molecule of ATP⁵. Pyruvate kinase is present in four distinct, tissue-specific isozymes in animals, each consisting of particular kinetic properties necessary to accommodate the variations in metabolic requirements of diverse tissues⁵. Pyruvate kinase also serves as a regulatory enzyme for gluconeogenesis, a biochemical pathway in which the liver generates glucose from pyruvate and other substrates⁵.
                -

            - Escherichia coli and Mycoplasma mycoides
                - Escherichia coli is a Gram-negative, facultative anaerobic, rod-shaped, coliform bacterium of the genus Escherichia that is commonly found in the lower intestine of warm-blooded organisms⁷. Although most strains of E. coli are harmless, others can make you sick⁵. Some kinds of E. coli can cause diarrhea, while others cause urinary tract infections⁹.
                - 
                - Mycoplasma mycoides is a bacterial species of the genus Mycoplasma in the class Mollicutes. This microorganism is a parasite that lives in ruminants. Mycoplasma mycoides comprises two subspecies, mycoides and capri, which infect cattle and small ruminants such as goats respectively[^10^]. Mycoplasma mycoides subsp. mycoides small colony (MmmSC) is the causative agent of contagious bovine pleuropneumonia (CBPP)⁴.

            - Regulatory proteins
                - Regulatory proteins are proteins that influence the regions of a DNA molecule that are transcribed by RNA polymerase during the process of transcription¹. These proteins, which include transcription factors, therefore help control the synthesis of proteins in cells¹. Regulatory proteins also play a role in muscle contraction and locomotion⁴. They protect their binding DNA sequences from nuclease cleavage, resulting in the markedly increased accessibility surrounding their binding sites and over neighboring chromatin³.
                - 
                - Source: Conversation with Bing, 4/2/2023(1) regulatory protein | Encyclopedia.com.         - ## Questions
            - What is JCVI-syn3A?
                - JCVI-syn3A
                    - Synthetic organism
                    - Created by synthesizing and reducing the genome of the natural parent Gram-positive organism Mycoplasma mycoides subsp. capri str. GM12
                    - Minimal cell with a reduced genome of 493 genes
                    - Retains few regulatory proteins or small RNAs
                    - Produces living cells that grow and divide in about 100 min
                    - Has consistent spherical morphologies with 400-500-nm diameters
                    - Presents a unique opportunity to develop a near-complete whole-cell kinetic model
            - What is a whole-cell fully dynamical kinetic model (WCM)?
                - Whole-cell fully dynamical kinetic model (WCM)
                    - Comprehensive model that considers the time-dependent behaviors of concentrations and reaction fluxes from a complete description of the state of a cell
                    - Treats metabolism, genetic information processes, and growth of a cell on a single-cell basis
                    - Requires synthesizing information from other well-studied organisms and -omics data such as metabolomics and proteomics to use as initial conditions
            - What is the role of cryo-electron tomograms in the development of a WCM?
                - Role of cryo-electron tomograms in the development of a WCM
                    - Provide the cell geometry and ribosome distributions at the single-cell level directly
                    - Used to construct an initial cell state in the development of a WCM
                    - Starting from the coordinates of the ribosomes and cell boundary, the DNA is folded around ribosomes as a circular self-avoiding polymer on a lattice in such a way that the sequence order and gene positions are maintained
                    - Each replicate cell uses the same ribosome coordinates from cryo-ET, but a different chromosome configuration unique among the ensemble
            - What do time-dependent behaviors of concentrations and reaction fluxes reveal about a cell?
                - Time-dependent behaviors of concentrations and reaction fluxes
                    - Reveal how the cell balances demands of its metabolism, genetic information processes, and growth
                    - Offer insight into the principles of life for this minimal cell
            - Why is the study of ‘‘minimal cells’’ important?
                - Importance of studying ‘‘minimal cells’’
                    - Helps design and build cellular genomes that do not include non-essential genes, which can reveal principles that apply to all living systems
                    - Allows gaining insight into the fundamental principles of life and testing understanding of the minimal requirements for life
                    - Provides a design tool for predicting the effects of changes in the genome
                    - Should be easier to completely describe than any known naturally occurring cells
            - What is required for a complete description of the state of a cell?
                - Complete description of cell state requires:
                    - Size and shape of the cell
                    - Components of the cell
                    - Intracellular reactions and interactions with environment
                    - all of these as a function of time and cell growth. 
                    - Theoretical models and simulations to interpret and integrate experimental data
            - What are the cellular components of JCVI-syn3A?
                - The cellular components of JCVI-syn3A include:
                    - Membrane glycolipid component
                    - Lipid bound capsular polysaccharide (CPS) component
                    - The CPS component is a part of its membrane architecture and is produced by M. mycoides, the parent organism of JCVI-syn3A. JCVI-syn3A produces a different type of CPS than M. mycoides, and it is unclear whether JCVI-syn3A produces a capsule.
                    - JCVI-syn3A has XXX genes involved in XXX metabolic reactions that are organized into seven subsystems: central, nucleotide, lipid, amino acid, cofactor, energy, and cell wall metabolism. These genes are essential for the organism's survival.
            - Why has simulating a complete description of the state of a cell been challenging?
                - Simulating a complete description of the state of a cell has been challenging due to the large numbers of genes with unknown function, the complexity in model systems such as Escherichia coli, and the broad range of concentrations and timescales that need to be considered. Additionally, establishing the reaction networks and the availability of kinetic parameters and -omics data such as metabolomics and proteomics to use as initial conditions are common challenges.
                - Challenges in simulating complete cell state description:
                    - Large number of genes with unknown functions
                    - Complexity in model systems such as E. coli
                    - Broad range of concentrations and timescales to be considered
                    - Establishing reaction networks and availability of kinetic parameters and -omics data as initial conditions
            - How have whole-cell models (WCMs) progressed over time?
                - Progression of whole-cell models (WCMs) over time:
                    - WCMs have advanced from genome-scale metabolic models (GSMMs) and steady-state flux calculations to fully dynamical kinetic models that interconnect subsystem networks and chemical species continuously over time on a single-cell basis.
                    - The most comprehensive WCMs have been developed for Mycoplasma genitalium and E. coli using ordinary differential equations, flux balance analysis, and stochastic simulations.
            - What are some common challenges in developing WCMs?
                - Establishing reaction networks and acquiring kinetic parameters and -omics data (e.g., metabolomics and proteomics) as initial conditions are common challenges in developing WCMs.
                - No single bacterium has a complete set of parameters and -omics data, so information must be synthesized from other well-studied organisms.
            - What are some -omics data that can be used as initial conditions in WCMs?
                - Metabolomics and proteomics are -omics data that can be used as initial conditions in WCMs.
                - However, these data may not be readily available, and synthesizing information from other well-studied organisms is often necessary as no single bacterium has a complete set of parameters and -omics data.
            - How many genes does JCVI-syn3A have in its genome?
                - JCVI-syn3A has 452 genes in its genome.
            - What are the demands that a cell balances in order to maintain its metabolism, genetic information processes, and growth?
                - The time-dependent behaviors of concentrations and reaction fluxes from stochastic-deterministic simulations over a cell cycle reveal how the cell balances demands of its metabolism, genetic information processes, and growth, and offer insight into the principles of life for this minimal cell. Homeostasis is a property of a normal cell to maintain constant intracellular concentrations over a cell cycle suggesting that upon experiencing a perturbation whether from the environment or an intracellular reaction, it responds by adjusting its biochemical pathways to bring the concentrations back into an acceptable range for stable functioning of its networks. The balance among use of ATP and GTP, nucleotide metabolism, and glycolysis is also important.
            - What is the difference between stochastic and deterministic simulations in the context of WCMs?
                - **Stochastic vs Deterministic Simulations in WCMs** 
                    - In the context of WCMs, deterministic simulations model the time-evolution of metabolite concentrations using ordinary differential equations (ODEs), while stochastic simulations use the chemical master equation (CME) or reaction-diffusion master equation (RDME) to model the distribution of system states and fluctuations.
                    - The advantage of a stochastic CME model over a deterministic ODE model is that the CME model reports the distribution of system states and fluctuations, rather than only the mean concentration. This is especially relevant for systems with low copy numbers, such as models of genetic information processing, where the fluctuations and mean are of a similar order of magnitude.
                    - The RDME is a spatially-resolved version of the CME, where the system is a set of connected sub-volumes and the system state is the distribution of particles within each sub-volume.
            - What is a genome-scale metabolic model (GSMM)?
                - **Genome-Scale Metabolic Model (GSMM)** 
                    - A genome-scale metabolic model (GSMM) is a computational model that describes the metabolic capabilities of an organism based on its genome sequence.
                    - It includes all the metabolic reactions that can occur in the organism and their associated enzymes, as well as the transporters that move metabolites across cellular membranes.
                    - GSMMs can be used to predict the growth rate and metabolic behavior of an organism under different conditions, and can be a useful tool for understanding the underlying biology of the organism.
            - What are some examples of organisms for which comprehensive WCMs have been developed?
                - Comprehensive WCMs have been developed for Mycoplasma genitalium and E. coli.
            - How can WCMs be used to predict the effects of changes in the genome?
                - According to the given context, "Such a model will also provide a design tool for predicting the effects of changes in the genome; for example, when a reaction pathway is added." Therefore, WCMs can be used as a design tool to predict the effects of changes in the genome, such as when a reaction pathway is added.
            - Why is establishing the reaction networks a common challenge in developing WCMs?
                - Establishing the reaction networks is a common challenge in developing WCMs because it requires the availability of kinetic parameters and -omics data such as metabolomics and proteomics to use as initial conditions. No one bacterium has a complete set of parameters and -omics data, so the development of a WCM relies upon synthesizing information from other well-studied organisms.
            - What is metabolomics and how is it used in WCMs?
                - Metabolomics is a type of -omics data that provides information on the concentrations of metabolites in a cell. In the development of whole-cell models (WCMs), metabolomics data is used as initial conditions for simulations. However, since no one bacterium has a complete set of parameters and -omics data, the development of a WCM relies upon synthesizing information from other well-studied organisms.
            - What is proteomics and how is it used in WCMs?
                - Proteomics is the large-scale study of proteins, particularly their structures and functions. In WCMs, proteomics is used to initialize protein counts for each gene in the model based on experimental data. In the simulations presented in the context, the protein counts were initialized to their counts from the genome-wide proteomics for Syn3A.
            - What are some potential applications of Whole cell models beyond understanding basic principles of living systems?
                - Sorry, I don't know how to help with that. The given context only discusses the development and challenges of whole-cell models (WCMs) and their progression from genome-scale metabolic models (GSMMs) to fully dynamical kinetic models. It also describes the development of a WCM for a minimal bacterial cell and its analysis, but it does not mention any potential applications of WCMs beyond understanding basic principles of living systems.
                - Whole cell models are computational representations of the entire molecular biology of a cell. They can be used to understand basic principles of living systems, such as how cells grow, divide, and respond to environmental changes. But they can also have many potential applications beyond that, such as:
                - 
                - Designing and engineering of reduced genomes, which could lead to new synthetic organisms with novel functions¹.
                - Designing and prototyping of cell-free systems, which could enable the production of biopharmaceuticals and biosensors without living cells¹.
                - Developing new strategies for drug discovery and personalized medicine, by simulating the effects of drugs on different cell types and conditions⁶.
                - Improving biotechnology and industrial processes, by optimizing metabolic pathways and cellular responses for higher yield and efficiency¹⁴.
                - Exploring the origins and evolution of life, by reconstructing ancestral genomes and simulating ancient environments⁶.
                - 
                - Whole cell models are still a developing field, but they have the potential to revolutionize our understanding and manipulation of biological systems.

            - What is the purpose of reducing the genome of JCVI-syn3A to a minimal number of genes?
                - **Reducing the Genome of JCVI-syn3A** 
                    - The purpose of reducing the genome of JCVI-syn3A to a minimal number of genes is to achieve a minimal genome producing living cells that grow, divide in about 100 min, and have consistent spherical morphologies with 400-500-nm diameters.
                    - This reduction in complexity and scale of Syn3A presents a unique opportunity to develop a near-complete whole-cell kinetic model.
                    - Additionally, Syn3A has a smaller fraction of genes with unclear function than other bacterial organisms, which makes it a good candidate for studying gene essentiality and proteomics.
            - What is the size of JCVI-syn3A compared to the model bacterial organism E. coli, and how was its genome synthesized?
                - **Size and Genome Synthesis of JCVI-syn3A** 
                    - JCVI-syn3A is approximately one-tenth of the physical size of E. coli.
                    - Its genome was synthesized based on the known genome sequence of the natural parent Gram-positive organism Mycoplasma mycoides subsp. capri str. GM12 (GenBank:CP001621.1) and has been synthetically reduced to achieve a minimal genome producing living cells that grow, divide in about 100 min, and have consistent spherical morphologies with 400-500-nm diameters.
            - How does JCVI-syn3A differ from its natural parent organism?
                - **Differences between JCVI-syn3A and its Natural Parent Organism** 
                    - JCVI-syn3A is a synthetic organism that was synthesized based on the known genome sequence of the natural parent Gram-positive organism Mycoplasma mycoides subsp. capri str. GM12.
                    - It has been synthetically reduced to achieve a minimal genome producing living cells that grow, divide in about 100 min, and have consistent spherical morphologies with 400-500-nm diameters.
                    - Its genome and physical size are approximately one-tenth those of the model bacterial organism E. coli.
                    - JCVI-syn3A has a smaller fraction of genes with unclear function (87/452, 20%) than E. coli (1780/4637, 38%) and Mycoplasma pneumoniae (311/688, 45%).

        - ## Key points
            - Unlike the previous whole-cell fully dynamical kinetic model (WCM), the simulations we present here are based on fully dynamical kinetic models where subsystem networks and chemical species are interconnected continuously over time on a single-cell basis
            - We report here the results from fully dynamical kinetic models, both for the well-stirred homogeneous (CME-ordinary differential equations (ODEs)) and 3D (RDME-chemical master equation (CME)-ODE) spatially resolved scenarios, for a living minimal bacterial cell
            - We provide the time-dependent information about the dynamic rates of genetic information processes, the 148 known metabolites, 452 proteins and mRNAs, 29 tRNAs, 503 ribosomes, and DNA undergoing over 7,000 reactions
            - The simulations based on the hybrid wellstirred (CME-ODE) whole-cell kinetic model have already given us quantitative insight into how the cell balances the demands of metabolism, genetic information, and growth over a cell cycle
            - The reaction-diffusion master equation (RDME)-CME-ODE simulations are currently limited by having the degradosome, RNAP, and ribosome complexes all initialized in inactive states
            - The highest cost in Syn3A is for metabolic reactions, in particular the PFK reaction in upper glycolysis using 75% of the total metabolic cost
            - No kinetic model for formation of FtsZ/FtsA filament and septum formation prior to formation of the daughter cells is considered. These processes will be addressed as we modify the spatially resolved kinetic model to allow a change in cell morphology, DNA replication, and ribosome biogenesis similar to the rule-based model we created for DNA replication in a slow-growing and dividing E. coli cell ([^Earnest_et+al_2016_a])
        - 
        - ## Synopsis
            - Dynamical model of a minimal cell that has retained few regulatory proteins or small RNAs and is capable of doubling in size.
            - The simulations are based on fully dynamical kinetic models where subsystem networks and chemical species are interconnected continuously over time on a single-cell basis.
            - No kinetic model for formation of FtsZ/FtsA filament and septum formation prior to formation of the daughter cells is considered.
            - Stochastic cell simulations are necessary when reactions involving few particles can lead to large variations in the state of the cell.
            - The authors present a whole-cell fully dynamical kinetic model (WCM) of JCVI-syn3A, a minimal cell with a reduced genome of 493 genes that has retained few regulatory proteins or small RNAs.
            - WCM reveals how emergent imbalances lead to slowdowns in the rates of transcription and translation.<br/><br/>Aspects of the researchers’ results appear to support prior work in this area: “The notion that the glycolipid biomass fraction stays approximately constant for the growth medium and membrane has been previously observed in M. pneumoniae,” Thornburg said. <br /><br />The investigators acknowledge that “RDME-CME-ODE simulations are limited by having the degradosome, RNAP, and ribosome complexes all initialized in inactive states.
            - No kinetic model for formation of FtsZ/FtsA filament and septum formation prior to formation of the daughter cells is considered.” <br /><br />The authors advocate that no experiments have been done to measure the number of active degradosomes in Syn3A and is a prediction to be validated in future experiments.
            - The predicted distribution of half-lives is in agreement with genome-wide studies carried out on related organisms.
            - <br /><br />Data and code to reproduce the analyses can be found at: <a target="_blank" href="https://doi.org/10.17632/nprw2h5tx6.1">https://doi.org/10.17632/nprw2h5tx6.1→a>, <a target="_blank" href="https://github.com/Luthey-Schulten-Lab/Lattice_Microbes">https://github.com/Luthey-Schulten-Lab/Lattice_Microbes↔a>, <a target="_blank" href="https://github.com">https://github.com↔a>.
